</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Gothic architecture</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Gothic style" redirects here. For the visual arts, see <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic art</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">

</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:ReimsCathedral0116.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/ReimsCathedral0116.jpg/300px-ReimsCathedral0116.jpg" width="300" height="190" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/ReimsCathedral0116.jpg/450px-ReimsCathedral0116.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/ReimsCathedral0116.jpg/600px-ReimsCathedral0116.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3965" data-file-height="2514" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">Aerial photo of Reims Cathedral looking south</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Gothic Architecture</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Architectural_style" title="Architectural style">architectural style</a> that flourished in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> during the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High</a> and <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late</a> <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. It evolved from <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque architecture</a> and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance architecture</a>. Originating in 12th century <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and lasting into the 16th century, Gothic architecture was known during the period as <i><span lang="la" xml:lang="la">Opus Francigenum</span></i> ("French work") with the term <i>Gothic</i> first appearing during the later part of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>. Its characteristics include the <a href="/wiki/Ogive" title="Ogive">pointed arch</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ribbed_vault" class="mw-redirect" title="Ribbed vault">ribbed vault</a> (which evolved from the joint vaulting of Romanesque architecture) and the <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying buttress</a>. Gothic architecture is most familiar as the architecture of many of the great <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedrals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abbey" title="Abbey">abbeys</a> and churches of Europe. It is also the architecture of many <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palace" title="Palace">palaces</a>, <a href="/wiki/Town_hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Town hall">town halls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guild halls</a>, universities and to a less prominent extent, private dwellings, such as dorms and rooms.</p>
<p>It is in the great churches and cathedrals and in a number of civic buildings that the Gothic style was expressed most powerfully, its characteristics lending themselves to appeals to the emotions, whether springing from faith or from civic pride. A great number of ecclesiastical buildings remain from this period, of which even the smallest are often structures of architectural distinction while many of the larger churches are considered priceless works of art and are listed with <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> as <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Sites</a>. For this reason a study of Gothic architecture is often largely a study of cathedrals and churches.</p>
<p>A series of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Gothic revivals</a> began in mid-18th century England, spread through 19th century Europe and continued, largely for ecclesiastical and university structures, into the 20th century.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Unlike with past and future styles of art, like the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_art" title="Carolingian art">Carolingian</a> style as noted by <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> art historian <a href="/wiki/Louis_Grodecki" title="Louis Grodecki">Louis Grodecki</a> in his work <i><a href="#CITEREFGrodecki1977">Gothic Architecture</a></i>, Gothic's lack of a definite historical or geographic nexus results in a weak concept of what truly is Gothic. This is further compounded by the fact that the technical, ornamentation, and formal features of Gothic are not entirely unique to it. Though modern historians have invariably accepted the conventional use of "Gothic" as a label, even in formal analysis processes due to a longstanding tradition of doing so, the definition of "Gothic" has historically varied wildly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Gothic_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Gothic (disambiguation)">Gothic</a> architecture" originated as a <a href="/wiki/Pejorative" title="Pejorative">pejorative</a> description. <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a> used the term "barbarous German style" in his 1550 <i><a href="/wiki/Lives_of_the_Most_Excellent_Painters,_Sculptors,_and_Architects" title="Lives of the Most Excellent Painters, Sculptors, and Architects">Lives of the Artists</a></i> to describe what is now considered the Gothic style,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVasari1991117.2C_527_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVasari1991117.2C_527-2">[2]</a></sup> and in the introduction to the <i>Lives</i> he attributes various architectural features to "<a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">the Goths</a>" whom he held responsible for destroying the ancient buildings after they conquered <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, and erecting new ones in this style.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVasariBrownMaclehose1907b._.26_83_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVasariBrownMaclehose1907b._.26_83-3">[3]</a></sup> Vasari was not alone among 15th and 16th Italian writers, as <a href="/wiki/Filarete" title="Filarete">Filarete</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giannozzo_Manetti" title="Giannozzo Manetti">Giannozzo Manetti</a> had also written scathing criticisms of the Gothic style, calling it a "barbaric prelude to the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>." Vasari and company were writing at a time when many aspects and vocabulary pertaining to Classical architecture had been reasserted with the Renaissance in the late 15th and 16th centuries, and they had the perspective that the "<i>maniera tedesca</i>" or "<i>maniera dei Goti</i>" was the antithesis of this resurgent style leading to the continuation of this negative connotation in the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Rabelais" title="François Rabelais">François Rabelais</a>, also of the 16th century, imagines an inscription over the door of his <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopian</a> <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Th%C3%A9l%C3%A8me" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbey of Thélème">Abbey of Thélème</a>, "Here enter no hypocrites, bigots..." slipping in a slighting reference to "Gotz" and "Ostrogotz."<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[a]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moli%C3%A8re" title="Molière">Molière</a> also made this note of the Gothic style in the 1669 poem <i>La Gloire</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><span class="languageicon">(in French)</span>: "...fade goût des ornements gothiques, Ces monstres odieux de siècles ignorants, Que de la barbarie ont produit les torrents.."<br />
<span class="languageicon">(in English)</span>: "...the insipid taste of Gothic ornamentation, these odious monstrosities of an ignorant age, produced by the torrents of barbarism..."</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Moli%C3%A8re" title="Molière">Molière</a>, <i>La Gloire</i></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In English 17th century usage, "Goth" was an equivalent of "<a href="/wiki/Vandal" class="mw-redirect" title="Vandal">vandal</a>," a savage despoiler with a Germanic heritage, and so came to be applied to the architectural styles of northern Europe from before the revival of classical types of architecture. According to a 19th-century correspondent in the London Journal <i><a href="/wiki/Notes_and_Queries" title="Notes and Queries">Notes and Queries</a></i>:<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>There can be no doubt that the term 'Gothic' as applied to pointed styles of ecclesiastical architecture was used at first contemptuously, and in derision, by those who were ambitious to imitate and revive the Grecian orders of architecture, after the revival of classical literature. Authorities such as <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">Christopher Wren</a> lent their aid in deprecating the old medieval style, which they termed Gothic, as synonymous with everything that was barbarous and rude.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The first movements that reevaluated medieval art took place in the 18th century,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779-1">[1]</a></sup> even when the <a href="/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_Royale_d%27Architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Académie Royale d'Architecture">Académie Royale d'Architecture</a> met in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> on 21 July 1710 and, amongst other subjects, discussed the new fashions of bowed and cusped arches on chimneypieces being employed to "finish the top of their openings. The Academy disapproved of several of these new manners, which are defective and which belong for the most part to the Gothic."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFiske194366_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFiske194366-6">[5]</a></sup> Despite resistance in the 19th and 20th centuries, such as the writings of <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Worringer" title="Wilhelm Worringer">Wilhelm Worringer</a>, critics like Père Laugier, William Gilpin, August Wilhelm Schlegel and other critics began to give the term a more positive meaning. <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a> called Gothic the "<i>deutsche Architektur</i>" and the "embodiment of German genius," while some French writers like <a href="/wiki/Camille_Enlart" title="Camille Enlart">Camille Enlart</a> instead nationalised it for France, dubbing it "<i>architecture français.</i>" This second group made some of their claims using the chronicle of Burchard von Halle that tells of the Church of <a href="/wiki/Bad_Wimpfen" title="Bad Wimpfen">Bad Wimpfen</a>'s construction "<i>opere francigeno</i>," or "in the French style." Today, the term is defined with spatial observations and historical and ideological information.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_and_scope">Definition and scope</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition and scope">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since the studies of the 18th century, many have attempted to define the Gothic style using a list of characteristic features, principally with the <a href="/wiki/Pointed_arch" class="mw-redirect" title="Pointed arch">pointed arch</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[b]</a></sup> the vaulting supported by intersecting arches, and the flying buttress. Eventually, historians composed a fairly large list of those features that were alien to both early medieval and Classical arts that includes piers with groups of colonettes, <a href="/wiki/Pinnacles" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinnacles">pinnacles</a>, gables, rose windows, and openings broken into many different lancet-shaped sections. Certain combinations thereof have been singled out for identifying regional or national sub-styles of Gothic or to follow the evolution of the style. From this emerge labels such as <a href="/wiki/Flamboyant_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="Flamboyant Gothic">Flamboyant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rayonnant" title="Rayonnant">Rayonnant</a>, and the English <a href="/wiki/Perpendicular_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="Perpendicular Gothic">Perpendicular</a> because of the observation of components like window tracery and pier moldings. This idea, dubbed by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Frankl" title="Paul Frankl">Paul Frankl</a> as "componential," had also occurred to mid 19th century writers such as <a href="/wiki/Arcisse_de_Caumont" title="Arcisse de Caumont">Arcisse de Caumont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Willis_(engineer)" title="Robert Willis (engineer)">Robert Willis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Mertens" title="Franz Mertens">Franz Mertens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[c]</a></sup></p>
<p>As an architectural style, Gothic developed primarily in ecclesiastical architecture, and its principles and characteristic forms were applied to other types of buildings. Buildings of every type were constructed in the Gothic style, with evidence remaining of simple domestic buildings, elegant town houses, grand palaces, commercial premises, civic buildings, castles, city walls, bridges, village churches, abbey churches, abbey complexes and large cathedrals.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The greatest number of surviving Gothic buildings are churches. These range from tiny chapels to large cathedrals, and although many have been extended and altered in different styles, a large number remain either substantially intact or sympathetically restored, demonstrating the form, character and decoration of Gothic architecture. The Gothic style is most particularly associated with the great cathedrals of Northern France, the Low Countries, England and Spain, with other fine examples occurring across Europe.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Beauvais_1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Beauvais_1.JPG/230px-Beauvais_1.JPG" width="230" height="173" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Beauvais_1.JPG/345px-Beauvais_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Beauvais_1.JPG/460px-Beauvais_1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political">Political</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Political">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The roots of the Gothic style lie in those towns that, since the 11th century, had been enjoying increased prosperity and growth, began to experience more and more freedom from traditional <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal</a> authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell19689_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell19689-10">[7]</a></sup> At the end of the 12th century, Europe was divided into a multitude of <a href="/wiki/City_state" class="mw-redirect" title="City state">city states</a> and kingdoms. The area encompassing modern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> and much of northern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> (excluding <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Papal_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Papal State">Papal State</a>) was nominally part of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, but local rulers exercised considerable autonomy under the system of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">Feudalism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1025%E2%80%931385)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1025–1385)">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Castile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">Aragon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Navarre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Cyprus" title="Kingdom of Cyprus">Cyprus</a> were independent kingdoms, as was the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">Angevin Empire</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet" title="House of Plantagenet">Plantagenet kings</a> ruled <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a> and large domains in what was to become modern <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[d]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> came under the influence of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, while the other Scandinavian countries and <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> were influenced by trading contacts with the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>. <a href="/wiki/Capetian_House_of_Anjou" title="Capetian House of Anjou">Angevin</a> kings brought the Gothic tradition from France to <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Lusignan" class="mw-redirect" title="Lusignan">Lusignan</a> kings introduced <a href="/wiki/French_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="French Gothic">French Gothic</a> architecture to <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>. Gothic art is sometimes viewed as the art of the era of feudalism but also as being connected to change in medieval social structure, as the Gothic style of architecture seemed to parallel the beginning of the decline of feudalism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720.2C_24_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720.2C_24-12">[8]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the influence of the established feudal elite can be seen in the <a href="/wiki/Chateaux" class="mw-redirect" title="Chateaux">Chateaux</a> of French lords and in those churches sponsored by feudal lords.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724-13">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout Europe at this time there was a rapid growth in trade and an associated growth in towns,<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JH1_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JH1-15">[11]</a></sup> and they would come to be predominate in Europe by the end of the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724-13">[9]</a></sup> Germany and the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a> had large flourishing towns that grew in comparative peace, in trade and competition with each other or united for mutual weal, as in the Hanseatic League. Civic building was of great importance to these towns as a sign of wealth and pride. England and France remained largely feudal and produced grand domestic architecture for their kings, dukes and <a href="/wiki/Bishops" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishops">bishops</a>, rather than grand <a href="/wiki/Town_hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Town hall">town halls</a> for their <a href="/wiki/Medieval_bourgeoisie" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval bourgeoisie">burghers</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Viollet-le-Duc contended that the blossoming of the Gothic style came about as a result of growing freedoms in construction professions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724-13">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious">Religious</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Religious">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The geographical expanse of the Gothic style is analogous to that of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, which prevailed across Europe at this time and influenced not only faith but also wealth and power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197724-13">[9]</a></sup> Bishops were appointed by the feudal lords (<a href="/wiki/King" title="King">Kings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke" title="Duke">Dukes</a>, and other landowners) and they often ruled as virtual princes over large estates. The early Medieval periods had seen a rapid growth in monasticism, with several different orders being prevalent and spreading their influence widely. Foremost were the <a href="/wiki/Benedictines" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictines">Benedictines</a> whose great abbey churches vastly outnumbered any others in France and England. A part of their influence was that towns developed around them and they became centers of culture, learning and commerce. The <a href="/wiki/Cluniacs" class="mw-redirect" title="Cluniacs">Cluniac</a> and Cistercian Orders were prevalent in France, the great monastery at <a href="/wiki/Cluny" title="Cluny">Cluny</a> having established a formula for a well planned monastic site which was then to influence all subsequent monastic building for many centuries. In the 13th century <a href="/wiki/St._Francis_of_Assisi" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Francis of Assisi">St. Francis of Assisi</a> established the <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscans</a>, a mendicant order. The <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominicans</a>, another mendicant order founded during the same period but by <a href="/wiki/St._Dominic" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Dominic">St. Dominic</a> in <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>, were particularly influential in the building of Italy's Gothic churches.<sup id="cite_ref-JH1_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JH1-15">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The primary use of the Gothic style is in religious structures, naturally leading it to an association with the Church and it is considered to be one of the most formal and coordinated forms of the physical church, thought of as being the physical residence of God on Earth. According to <a href="/wiki/Hans_Sedlmayr" title="Hans Sedlmayr">Hans Sedlmayr</a>, it was "even the considered the temporal image of <a href="/wiki/Paradise" title="Paradise">Paradise</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/New_Jerusalem" title="New Jerusalem">New Jerusalem</a>." The horizontal and vertical scope of the Gothic church, filled with the light thought of as a symbol of the grace of God admitted into the structure via the style's iconic windows are among the very best examples of Christian architecture. <a href="/wiki/Louis_Grodecki" title="Louis Grodecki">Grodecki</a>'s <i><a href="#CITEREFGrodecki1977">Gothic Architecture</a></i> also notes that the glass pieces of various colors that make up those windows have been compared to "precious stones encrusting the walls of the New Jerusalem," and that "the numerous towers and pinnacles evoke similar structures that appear in the visions of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">Saint John</a>." Another idea, held by <a href="/wiki/Georg_Dehio" title="Georg Dehio">Georg Dehio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Panofsky" title="Erwin Panofsky">Erwin Panofsky</a>, is that the designs of Gothic followed the current theological scholastic thought.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720-16">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> show <a href="/wiki/Nova_(TV_series)" title="Nova (TV series)">NOVA</a> explored the influence of the Holy Bible in the dimensions and design of some cathedrals.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic">Geographic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geographic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the 10th to the 13th century, <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque architecture</a> had become a pan-European style and manner of construction, affecting buildings in countries as far apart as <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>. The same wide geographic area was then affected by the development of Gothic architecture, but the acceptance of the Gothic style and methods of construction differed from place to place, as did the expressions of Gothic taste. The proximity of some regions meant that modern country borders did not define divisions of style. On the other hand, some regions such as <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> produced defining characteristics rarely seen elsewhere, except where they have been carried by itinerant craftsmen, or the transfer of bishops.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Many different factors like geographical/geological, economic, social, or political situations caused the regional differences in the great abbey churches and cathedrals of the Romanesque period that would often become even more apparent in the Gothic. For example, studies of the population statistics reveals disparities such as the multitude of churches, abbeys, and cathedrals in northern <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> while in more urbanised regions construction activity of a similar scale was reserved to a few important cities. Such an example comes from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Roberto_L%C3%B3pez&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Roberto López (page does not exist)">Roberto López</a>, wherein the French city of <a href="/wiki/Amiens" title="Amiens">Amiens</a> was able to fund its architectural projects whereas <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> could not because of the economic inequality of the two.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197725-26_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197725-26-18">[14]</a></sup> This wealth, concentrated in rich monasteries and noble families, would eventually spread certain Italian, Catalan, and Hanseatic bankers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197725_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197725-19">[15]</a></sup> This would be amended when the economic hardships of the 13th century were no longer felt, allowing <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>, and the southern <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a> to enter into competition with France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197726_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197726-20">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The local availability of materials affected both construction and style. In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> was readily available in several grades, the very fine white limestone of <a href="/wiki/Caen" title="Caen">Caen</a> being favoured for sculptural decoration. England had coarse limestone and red <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> as well as dark green <a href="/wiki/Purbeck_marble" class="mw-redirect" title="Purbeck marble">Purbeck marble</a> which was often used for architectural features. In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Germany" title="Northern Germany">Northern</a> <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, and the Baltic countries local building stone was unavailable but there was a strong tradition of building in brick. The resultant style, <a href="/wiki/Brick_Gothic" title="Brick Gothic">Brick Gothic</a>, is called <i><a href="/wiki/Backsteingotik" class="mw-redirect" title="Backsteingotik">Backsteingotik</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> and is associated with the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, stone was used for fortifications, so brick was preferred for other buildings. Because of the extensive and varied deposits of marble, many buildings were faced in marble, or were left with undecorated façade so that this might be achieved at a later date. The availability of timber also influenced the style of architecture, with timber buildings prevailing in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>. Availability of timber affected methods of roof construction across Europe. It is thought that the magnificent <a href="/wiki/Hammerbeam" class="mw-redirect" title="Hammerbeam">hammerbeam</a> roofs of England were devised as a direct response to the lack of long straight seasoned timber by the end of the Medieval period, when forests had been decimated not only for the construction of vast roofs but also for ship building.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CT_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CT-21">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_Eastern_influence">Possible Eastern influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Possible Eastern influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:527px;max-width:527px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:167px;max-width:167px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:C_(203).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/C_%28203%29.JPG/165px-C_%28203%29.JPG" width="165" height="124" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/C_%28203%29.JPG/248px-C_%28203%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/C_%28203%29.JPG/330px-C_%28203%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Delal" class="mw-redirect" title="Delal">Delal Bridge</a>, Iraq</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:167px;max-width:167px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Ar%5ERaqqa_SYRIE_324.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Ar%5ERaqqa_SYRIE_324.jpg/165px-Ar%5ERaqqa_SYRIE_324.jpg" width="165" height="124" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Ar%5ERaqqa_SYRIE_324.jpg/248px-Ar%5ERaqqa_SYRIE_324.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Ar%5ERaqqa_SYRIE_324.jpg/330px-Ar%5ERaqqa_SYRIE_324.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Arches at <a href="/wiki/Al-Raqqah" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Raqqah">Al-Raqqah</a>, Syria</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:187px;max-width:187px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Monreale-bjs-17.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Monreale-bjs-17.jpg/185px-Monreale-bjs-17.jpg" width="185" height="123" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Monreale-bjs-17.jpg/278px-Monreale-bjs-17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Monreale-bjs-17.jpg/370px-Monreale-bjs-17.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1024" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Monreale_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Monreale Cathedral">Monreale Cathedral</a>, Sicily</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The pointed arch, one of the defining attributes of Gothic, was earlier incorporated into <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic conquest of Syria">Islamic conquests of</a> <a href="/wiki/Roman_Syria" title="Roman Syria">Roman Syria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Empire</a> in the 7th century.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup> The pointed arch and its precursors had been employed in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Architecture">Late Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanian</a> architecture; within the Roman context, evidenced in early church building in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and occasional secular structures, like the Roman <a href="/wiki/Karamagara_Bridge" title="Karamagara Bridge">Karamagara Bridge</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Sassanid" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid">Sassanid</a> architecture, in the parabolic and pointed arches employed in palace and sacred construction.<sup id="cite_ref-Warren1991_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warren1991-22">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Increasing military and cultural contacts with the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_southern_Italy" title="Norman conquest of southern Italy">Norman conquest</a> of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam_in_southern_Italy" title="History of Islam in southern Italy">Islamic Sicily</a> in 1090, the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> (beginning 1096), and the <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Islamic presence in Spain</a>, may have influenced <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Europe">Medieval Europe</a>'s adoption of the pointed arch, although this hypothesis remains controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott2003113_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott2003113-24">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBony198317_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBony198317-25">[21]</a></sup> Certainly, in those parts of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Mediterranean">Western Mediterranean</a> subject to Islamic control or influence, rich regional variants arose, fusing <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> and later Gothic traditions with <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic</a> decorative forms, for example in <a href="/wiki/Monreale_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Monreale Cathedral">Monreale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cefal%C3%B9_Cathedral" title="Cefalù Cathedral">Cefalù Cathedrals</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Alc%C3%A1zar_of_Seville" title="Alcázar of Seville">Alcázar of Seville</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Teruel_Cathedral" title="Teruel Cathedral">Teruel Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of scholars have cited the <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenian</a> <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Ani" title="Cathedral of Ani">Cathedral of Ani</a>, completed 1001 or 1010, as a possible influence on the Gothic, especially due to its use of pointed arches and cluster piers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELang1980223_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELang1980223-27">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart195980_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart195980-29">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbot_Rice1972179_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbot_Rice1972179-30">[26]</a></sup> However, other scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenian</a> <a href="/wiki/Art_historian" class="mw-redirect" title="Art historian">art historian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sirarpie_Der_Nersessian" title="Sirarpie Der Nersessian">Sirarpie Der Nersessian</a>, who rejected this notion as she argued that the pointed arches did not serve the same function of supporting the vault.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGarso.C3.AFan2015300_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGarso.C3.AFan2015300-31">[27]</a></sup> Furthermore, the website Virtual Ani (now defunct) wrote that there is "no evidence to indicate that there was a connection between Armenian architecture and the development of the Gothic style in Western Europe."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup> Regardless, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lucy_Der_Manuelian&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lucy Der Manuelian (page does not exist)">Lucy Der Manuelian</a>, author of <i>Ani: The Fabled Capital of Armenia</i>, contends that some <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a> (historically documented as being in Western Europe in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197737_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197737-33">[29]</a></sup> could have brought the knowledge and technique employed at Ani to the west.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDer_Manuelian20017_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDer_Manuelian20017-34">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The view held by the majority of scholars however is that the pointed arch evolved naturally in Western Europe as a structural solution to a technical problem, with evidence for this being its use as a stylistic feature in Romanesque French and English churches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott2003113_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott2003113-24">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:174px;max-width:174px">

</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:159px;max-width:159px">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:235px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Cefalu_Cathedral_interior_BW_2012-10-11_12-07-53.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Cefalu_Cathedral_interior_BW_2012-10-11_12-07-53.jpg/157px-Cefalu_Cathedral_interior_BW_2012-10-11_12-07-53.jpg" width="157" height="236" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Cefalu_Cathedral_interior_BW_2012-10-11_12-07-53.jpg/236px-Cefalu_Cathedral_interior_BW_2012-10-11_12-07-53.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Cefalu_Cathedral_interior_BW_2012-10-11_12-07-53.jpg/314px-Cefalu_Cathedral_interior_BW_2012-10-11_12-07-53.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2310" data-file-height="3470" /></a></div>

</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Gothic style originated in the <a href="/wiki/Ile-de-France" class="mw-redirect" title="Ile-de-France">Ile-de-France</a> region of France at the <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> era in the first half of the 12th century,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102-35">[31]</a></sup> at the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Sens" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of Sens">Cathedral of Sens</a> (1130–62) and <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_St-Denis" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbey of St-Denis">Abbey of St-Denis</a> (c. 1130–40 and 1140–44),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197736_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197736-36">[32]</a></sup> and did not immediately supersede it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102-35">[31]</a></sup> An example of this lack clean break is the blossoming of the Late Romanesque (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Spätromanisch</i></span>) in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hohenstaufen" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Hohenstaufen">Hohenstaufens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a> while the Gothic style spread into England and France in the 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197726_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197726-20">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Romanesque_tradition">Romanesque tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Romanesque tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque architecture</a></div>
<p>By the 12th century, <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque architecture</a>, termed <a href="/wiki/Norman_architecture" title="Norman architecture">Norman Gothic</a> in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup> was established throughout <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and provided the basic architectural forms and units that were to remain in evolution throughout the Medieval period. The important categories of building: the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parish_church" title="Parish church">parish church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palace" title="Palace">palace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_hall" title="Great hall">great hall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gatehouse" title="Gatehouse">gatehouse</a>, and civic building had been established in the Romanesque period.</p>
<p>Many architectural features that are associated with Gothic architecture had been developed and used by the architects of Romanesque buildings, but not fully exploited.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980104_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980104-38">[34]</a></sup> These include <a href="/wiki/Ribbed_vault" class="mw-redirect" title="Ribbed vault">ribbed</a> <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaults</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buttresses" class="mw-redirect" title="Buttresses">buttresses</a>, clustered columns, <a href="/wiki/Ambulatory" title="Ambulatory">ambulatories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rose_window" title="Rose window">wheel windows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spire" title="Spire">spires</a>, stained glass windows, and richly carved door <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NP_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NP-39">[35]</a></sup> These features, namely the rib vault and the pointed arch, had been used since the late 11th century in Southern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Picardy" title="Picardy">Picardy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980104_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980104-38">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was principally the widespread introduction of a single feature, the pointed arch, which was to bring about the change that separates Gothic from Romanesque. The technological change permitted a stylistic change which broke the tradition of massive masonry and solid walls penetrated by small openings, replacing it with a style where light appears to triumph over substance. With its use came the development of many other architectural devices, previously put to the test in scattered buildings and then called into service to meet the structural, aesthetic and ideological needs of the new style. These include the flying buttresses, pinnacles and traceried windows which typify Gothic ecclesiastical architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_from_Romanesque_to_Gothic_architecture">Transition from Romanesque to Gothic architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Transition from Romanesque to Gothic architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gothic architecture did not emerge from a dying Romanesque tradition, but from a Romanesque style at the height of its popularity, and it would supplant it for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102-35">[31]</a></sup> This shift in style beginning in the mid 12th century came about in an environment of much intellectual and political development as the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> began to grow into a very powerful political entity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102-03_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980102-03-40">[36]</a></sup> Another transition made by Gothic was the move from the rural monasteries of the Romanesque into urban environments with new Gothic churches built in wealthy cities by secular clergy knowing full well the growing unity and power of the Church.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980103_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980103-41">[37]</a></sup> The characteristic forms that were to define Gothic architecture grew out of Romanesque architecture and developed at several different geographic locations, as the result of different influences and structural requirements. While barrel vaults and groin vaults are typical of Romanesque architecture, ribbed vaults were used in many later Romanesque churches. The first examples of the ribbed vault, atop the thick walls of the Romanesque church, appeared at the same time in England and Normandy at Durham Cathedral (from 1093-before 1110), <a href="/wiki/Winchester,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Winchester, England">Winchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">Peterborough</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">Gloucester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lessay" title="Lessay">Lessay</a> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lessay_Abbey&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lessay Abbey (page does not exist)">Abbey</a>'s choir and transept, <a href="/wiki/Duclair" title="Duclair">Duclair</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_Saint_Paul,_Rouen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Church of Saint Paul, Rouen (page does not exist)">Church of Saint Paul</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197737_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197737-33">[29]</a></sup> The geometric ornamentation borne by the moldings of some of these vaults attests to the want for more decoration, and this would be answered later by architects working in <a href="/wiki/Ile-de-France" class="mw-redirect" title="Ile-de-France">Ile-de-France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valois_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Valois France">Valois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vexin" title="Vexin">Vexin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197737.2C_39_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197737.2C_39-42">[38]</a></sup> Later French projects from 1125 to 1135 show the lightening up of vaults contoured in a single or double convex profile and thinner walls. The Abbey of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre_Dame_de_Morienval&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Notre Dame de Morienval (page does not exist)">Notre Dame de Morienval</a> in Valois is one such example, with vaulting covering trapezoidal around an ambulatory, lightened supports and vaulting that would be copied at <a href="/wiki/Sens_Cathedral" title="Sens Cathedral">Sens Cathedral</a> and Suger's <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint-Denis" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of Saint-Denis">Basilica of Saint-Denis</a>. While Norman architects would also participate in this development, the Romanesque in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a> would remain the same with only little experimentation with vaulting. Two more features of Norman Romanesque, the wall buttress and the thick "double shell" wall at window height, were to later play a role in the birth of Gothic architecture. This double wall, a convenient way to reach the windows, hosted a passageway of recycled space that first appeared in the transepts of <a href="/wiki/Bernay,_Eure" title="Bernay, Eure">Bernay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jumi%C3%A8ges_Abbey" title="Jumièges Abbey">Jumièges Abbey</a> around 1040-50. This window-level passageway gave an illusion of weightlessness, inspired <a href="/wiki/Noyon_Cathedral" title="Noyon Cathedral">Noyon Cathedral</a>, and would affect the entirety of the Gothic form of art.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197739_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197739-43">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other characteristics of early Gothic architecture, such as vertical shafts, clustered columns, compound piers, plate tracery and groups of narrow openings had evolved during the Romanesque period. The west front of <a href="/wiki/Ely_Cathedral" title="Ely Cathedral">Ely Cathedral</a> exemplifies this development. Internally the three tiered arrangement of arcade, gallery and clerestory was established. Interiors had become lighter with the insertion of more and larger windows.</p>
<p>All modern historians agree that Suger's St.-Denis and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Sanglier" title="Henri Sanglier">Henri Sanglier</a>'s Sens Cathedral exemplify the development of Norman Romanesque architectural features into the Gothic through a new ordering of interior space, accented by support from supports freestanding and otherwise, and the shift of emphasis from sheer size to admittance of light. Later additions or remodeling prevent the observation of either structure in the time of their construction, the original plan was nonetheless recreated the plans of each and, as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Francis_Salet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Francis Salet (page does not exist)">Francis Salet</a> points out, Sens (the older of the two) still uses a Romanesque plan with an ambulatory and no transept and echoes with its supports the old Norman alternations. Its three-story high pointed arcade, openings above the vaulting, and windows are not derived from Burgundy, but rather from the triple division present in Normandy and England. Even the sexpartite vaulting of Sens's nave is likely of Norman origin, though the presence of wall ribbing belies Burgundian influence in design. Sens would, in spite of its archaic Norman features, exert much influence. From Sens spread the shrinking or omitting of the transept, the sexpartite vault, alternating interior, and the three-story elevation of future churches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197741_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197741-44">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abbot_Suger">Abbot Suger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Abbot Suger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:176px;max-width:176px">

</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:169px;max-width:169px">

</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The beginning of the Gothic style is held by all modern historians to be in the first half of the 12th century at the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_St_Denis" title="Basilica of St Denis">Basilica of St Denis</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ile-de-France" class="mw-redirect" title="Ile-de-France">Ile-de-France</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197741_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197741-44">[40]</a></sup> the royal domain of the <a href="/wiki/Capetian_dynasty" title="Capetian dynasty">Capetian</a> kings rich in industry and the wool trade,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell196811_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell196811-45">[41]</a></sup> because of the records he left during reconstruction of what he desired of this renovation, rather than the contemporary churches that explore some of the same ideas used at St. Denis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980103_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980103-41">[37]</a></sup> Suger believed in the spiritual power of light and colour, following in the philosophy of the 3rd century pagan <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_the_Areopagite" title="Dionysius the Areopagite">Dionysius the Areopagite</a>, whose identity was fused with that of the <a href="/wiki/Genevieve" title="Genevieve">patron saint of</a> <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, and leading him in the end to require large windows of stained glass.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[e]</a></sup> This new church also needed to be larger than the previous <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_architecture" title="Carolingian architecture">Carolingian</a> building to allow a greater number of pilgrims to feast inside the church.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980103-04_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980103-04-46">[42]</a></sup> The solution, Suger found, was to make unprecedented use of the <a href="/wiki/Ribbed_vault" class="mw-redirect" title="Ribbed vault">ribbed</a> <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vault</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pointed_arch" class="mw-redirect" title="Pointed arch">pointed arch</a>. St. Denis's plan possesses some very irregular shapes in its bays, prompting its architect to build the arches first so that arches of different height had keystones at the same height. Next the infill was added, and this method was proven to both provide more visual stimulation and speed up construction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980104_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaeburn1980104-38">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The choir and west front of the Abbey of Saint-Denis both became the prototypes for further building in the royal domain of northern France and in the Duchy of Normandy. Through the rule of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Plantagenet" title="House of Plantagenet">Angevin dynasty</a>, the new style was introduced to <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and spread throughout France, the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>.</p>
<p>Compared to <a href="/wiki/Sens_Cathedral" title="Sens Cathedral">Sens Cathedral</a>, St.-Denis is more complex and innovative. There is an obvious difference between the enclosing ambulatory around the choir, dedicated 11 June 1144 in the presence of the King,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[f]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JH1_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JH1-15">[11]</a></sup> and the pre-Suger <a href="/wiki/Narthex" title="Narthex">narthex</a>, or antenave, (1140) that is derived from pre-<a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> <a href="/wiki/Ottonian_architecture" title="Ottonian architecture">Ottonian</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Westwerk" class="mw-redirect" title="Westwerk">Westwerk</a></i>, and it shows in the heavily molded cross-ribbing and multiple projecting colonnettes positioned directly under the <a href="/wiki/Volute" title="Volute">volutes</a> of the rib's <a href="/wiki/Archivolts" class="mw-redirect" title="Archivolts">archivolts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197741.2C_43_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197741.2C_43-50">[44]</a></sup> However, in iconographical terms, the three portals display, for the first time, sculpture that is demonstrably no longer Romanesque.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197741_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197741-44">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread">Spread</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Spread">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Even as the role of the monastic orders seemed to diminish in the dawn of the Gothic era, the orders still had their own parts to play in the spread of the Gothic style, also disproving the common evaluation of <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> as the rural monastic style and Gothic as the urban ecclesiastical style. Chief among early promoters of this style were the <a href="/wiki/Benedictine_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine order">Benedictines</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">France</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Normandy" title="Duchy of Normandy">Normandy</a>. Gothic churches that can be associated with them include <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham Cathedral</a> in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_St_Denis" title="Basilica of St Denis">Abbey of St Denis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vezelay_Abbey" class="mw-redirect" title="Vezelay Abbey">Vezelay Abbey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Saint-Remi" title="Abbey of Saint-Remi">Abbey of Saint-Remi</a> in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>. Later Benedictine projects (constructions and renovations), made possible by the continued prominence of the Benedictine order throughout the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Age">Middle Ages</a>, include <a href="/wiki/Reims" title="Reims">Reims</a>'s <a href="/w/index.php?title=Abbey_of_Saint-Nicaise&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Abbey of Saint-Nicaise (page does not exist)">Abbey of Saint-Nicaise</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St._Ouen,_Rouen" title="Church of St. Ouen, Rouen">Abbey of Saint-Ouen</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Abbey_of_Chaise-Dieu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Abbey of Chaise-Dieu (page does not exist)">Abbey of St. Robert</a> at <a href="/wiki/La_Chaise-Dieu" title="La Chaise-Dieu">La Chaise-Dieu</a>, and the choir of <a href="/wiki/Mont_Saint-Michel" title="Mont Saint-Michel">Mont Saint-Michel</a> in France; English examples are <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, and the reconstruction of <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">the Benedictine church at</a> <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">Canterbury</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Cistercian_Order" class="mw-redirect" title="Cistercian Order">Cistercians</a> also had a hand in the spread of the Gothic style, first utilizing the Romanesque style for their monasteries since their inception as a reflection of their poverty, they became the total disseminators of the Gothic style as far east and south as <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197728_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197728-51">[45]</a></sup> Smaller orders, the <a href="/wiki/Carthusian_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Carthusian order">Carthusians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Premonstratensians" title="Premonstratensians">Premonstratensians</a>, also built some 200 churches (usually near cities),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197729-30_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197729-30-52">[46]</a></sup> but it was the mendicant orders, the <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominicans</a>, who would most affect the change of art from the Romanesque to the Gothic in the 13th and 14th centuries. Of the military orders, the <a href="/wiki/Knights_Templar" title="Knights Templar">Knights Templar</a> did not much contribute while the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Order" title="Teutonic Order">Teutonic Order</a> spread Gothic art into <a href="/wiki/Pomerania" title="Pomerania">Pomerania</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Prussia" title="East Prussia">East Prussia</a>, and the Baltic region.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197730_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197730-53">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics_of_the_Gothic_style">Characteristics of the Gothic style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Characteristics of the Gothic style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gotic3d2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Gotic3d2.jpg/250px-Gotic3d2.jpg" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Gotic3d2.jpg/375px-Gotic3d2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Gotic3d2.jpg/500px-Gotic3d2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gotic3d2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Various elements of Gothic architecture highlighted in red.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While many secular buildings exist from the Late Middle Ages, it is in the cathedrals and great churches that Gothic architecture displays its pertinent structures and characteristics to the fullest advantage. 19th century art historians and critics, used to the Baroque or Neoclassical works of the 17th and 18th centuries, were astounded by the soaring heights of a Gothic cathedral and made note of the extreme length compared to proportionally modest width and accentuating clusters of support colonnettes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197713_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197713-54">[48]</a></sup> This emphasis on verticality and light was applied to an <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical">ecclesiastical</a> building was achieved by the development of certain architectural features of the Gothic style that, when together, provided inventive solutions to various engineering problems. As <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a> observed, the Gothic cathedral, almost always laid out in a cruciform shape, was based on a logical skeleton of clustered <a href="/wiki/Column" title="Column">columns</a>, pointed <a href="/wiki/Ribbed_vault" class="mw-redirect" title="Ribbed vault">ribbed</a> <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaults</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying</a> <a href="/wiki/Buttress" title="Buttress">buttresses</a> arranged in a system of diagonal arches and arches enclosing the vault field that allows the outward thrust exerted by the groin vaults to be channeled from the walls and into specific points on a supporting mass. The result of this curvature in the vaults and arches of the church was the casting of indeterminable localised thrust that architects learned to counter with an opposing thrust in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying buttress</a> and application of calculated weight via the <a href="/wiki/Pinnacle" title="Pinnacle">pinnacle</a>. This dynamic system of various constituent elements filling a certain role allowed for the slimming of previously massive walls or replacement thereof with windows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197711_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197711-8">[6]</a></sup> Gothic churches were also very ornamented and highly decorated, serving as a <a href="/wiki/Poor_Man%27s_Bible" title="Poor Man's Bible">Poor Man's Bible</a> and a record of their construction in the stained glass windows that admit light into the church interior and some of the <a href="/wiki/Gargoyles" class="mw-redirect" title="Gargoyles">gargoyles</a>. These structures, for centuries the principle landmark in a town, would then often be surmounted by one or more <a href="/wiki/Tower" title="Tower">towers</a> and pinnacles and perhaps tall <a href="/wiki/Spire" title="Spire">spires</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swaan_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swaan-55">[49]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tnone center">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:852px;max-width:852px">
