</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pointed_arch">Pointed arch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Pointed arch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ogive" title="Ogive">Ogive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rib_vault" title="Rib vault">Rib vault</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canterbury_Cathedral_11_norman_arcade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_11_norman_arcade.jpg/220px-Canterbury_Cathedral_11_norman_arcade.jpg" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_11_norman_arcade.jpg/330px-Canterbury_Cathedral_11_norman_arcade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_11_norman_arcade.jpg/440px-Canterbury_Cathedral_11_norman_arcade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1214" data-file-height="708" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Canterbury_Cathedral_11_norman_arcade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Norman <a href="/wiki/Blind_arcade" title="Blind arcade">blind-arcading</a> at <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oleviste_kirik2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Oleviste_kirik2.JPG/220px-Oleviste_kirik2.JPG" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Oleviste_kirik2.JPG/330px-Oleviste_kirik2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Oleviste_kirik2.JPG/440px-Oleviste_kirik2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3072" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oleviste_kirik2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
15th century rib vaulting in <a href="/wiki/St._Olaf%27s_Church,_Tallinn" title="St. Olaf's Church, Tallinn">St. Olaf's Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tallinn" title="Tallinn">Tallinn</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One of the defining characteristics of Gothic architecture is the pointed (or <a href="/wiki/Ogive" title="Ogive">ogival</a>) arch, and it is used in nearly all places a vaulted shape might be called for structural or decorative consideration, like doorways, windows, arcades, and galleries. Gothic <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaulting</a> above spaces, regardless of size, is sometimes supported by richly moulded ribs. The constant use of the pointed arch in Gothic arches and tracery eventually led to the creation of the now extinct term "<i>ogival architecture</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki19779-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The pointed arch is also a characteristic feature of <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near Eastern</a> pre-Islamic <a href="/wiki/Sassanian_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanian architecture">Sassanian architecture</a> that was adopted in the 7th century by <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a> and appears in structures like the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan" title="Great Mosque of Kairouan">Great Mosque</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kairouan" title="Kairouan">Kairouan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mosque%E2%80%93Cathedral_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Mosque–Cathedral of Córdoba">Mosque–Cathedral</a> of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoba</a>, and several structures of <a href="/wiki/Norman_Sicily" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Sicily">Norman Sicily</a>. Then, it appeared in some <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> works in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Modena" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of Modena">Cathedral of Modena</a>) <a href="/wiki/Burgundy" title="Burgundy">Burgundy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Autun_Cathedral" title="Autun Cathedral">Autun Cathedral</a>), later being mastered by Gothic architects for the cathedrals of <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">Notre-Dame</a> <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">de Paris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Noyon_Cathedral" title="Noyon Cathedral">Noyon Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197736_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197736-36">[32]</a></sup> The majority view of scholars however is the idea that the pointed arch was a simultaneous and natural evolution in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western</a> <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> as a solution to the problem of <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaulting</a> spaces of irregular plan, or to bring transverse vaults to the same height as diagonal vaults,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott2003113_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott2003113-24">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Durndig_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Durndig-57">[50]</a></sup> as evidenced by <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham Cathedral</a>'s nave aisles, built in 1093. Pointed arches also occur extensively in Romanesque decorative blind arcading, where semi-circular arches overlap each other in a simple decorative pattern and their points an accident in design.<sup id="cite_ref-Durndig_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Durndig-57">[50]</a></sup> In addition to being able to its applicability to rectangular or irregular shapes, the pointed arch channels weight onto the bearing piers or columns at a steep angle, enabling architects to raise vaults much higher than was possible in Romanesque architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup> When used with other typical features of Gothic construction, a system of mutual independence in dispensing the immense weight of a Gothic cathedral's roof and vaulting emerges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197711_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197711-8">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rows of pointed arches upon delicate shafts form a typical wall decoration known as blind arcading. Niches with pointed arches and containing statuary are a major external feature. The pointed arch lent itself to elaborate intersecting shapes which developed within window spaces into complex Gothic tracery forming the structural support of the large windows that are characteristic of the style.<sup id="cite_ref-CT_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CT-21">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NP_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NP-39">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ribbed vault, another key feature of the Gothic style, has a history just as colorful, having long been adapted for the Roman (<a href="/wiki/Villa_of_the_sette_bassi" title="Villa of the sette bassi">Villa of Sette Bassi</a>), Sassanian, Islamic (<a href="/wiki/Abbas_I_of_Persia#Isfahan:_a_new_capital" title="Abbas I of Persia">Abbas I's Mosque at Isfahan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Cristo_de_la_Luz" title="Mosque of Cristo de la Luz">Mosque of Cristo de la Luz</a>), Romanesque (<a href="/wiki/L%27H%C3%B4pital-Saint-Blaise" title="L'Hôpital-Saint-Blaise">L'Hôpital-Saint-Blaise</a>), and then Gothic styles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197736_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197736-36">[32]</a></sup> Until the height of the Gothic era, few Western rib vaults matched the complexity of Islamic (mostly Moorish), beginning with experiments in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, from the 10th to the 13th centuries, such as ribbed domes (<a href="/wiki/Ani_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Ani Cathedral">Ani Cathedral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikortsminda_Cathedral" title="Nikortsminda Cathedral">Nikortsminda Cathedral</a>), diagonal arches on a square field (Ani), and arches perpendicular to walls (Homoros Vank). However, the function of these vaults is entirely structural rather than decorative, as in Gothic cathedrals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197736-37_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197736-37-58">[51]</a></sup> However, their indrect method of supporting the vault via its shoulders has been found at <a href="/wiki/Casale_Monferrato" title="Casale Monferrato">Casale Monferrato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_d%27%C3%89tampes" title="Château d'Étampes">Tour Guinette</a>, and at a tower at <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Cathedral" title="Bayeux Cathedral">Bayeux Cathedral</a>. One reason for this perhaps is the record of economic and political exchange between some of western Europe and Armenia, which might explain the similarities between Armenian architecture and the ribbed vaults at <a href="/wiki/San_Nazzaro_Sesia" title="San Nazzaro Sesia">San Nazzaro Sesia</a> and at <a href="/wiki/Lodi_Vecchio" title="Lodi Vecchio">Lodi Vecchio</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a> and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Abbey_of_Saint_Aubin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Abbey of Saint Aubin (page does not exist)">Abbey of Saint Aubin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Angers" title="Angers">Angers</a>. Ribbed vaults saw something of a golden age of development in the Anglo-Norman period, and led to the establishment of <a href="/wiki/French_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="French Gothic">French Gothic</a> and outlined many future Gothic solutions to the problem of support with buttresses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki1977p._37_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki1977p._37-59">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Height">Height</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Height">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki-01-Choir-of-Cologne-Cathedral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Wiki-01-Choir-of-Cologne-Cathedral.jpg/220px-Wiki-01-Choir-of-Cologne-Cathedral.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Wiki-01-Choir-of-Cologne-Cathedral.jpg/330px-Wiki-01-Choir-of-Cologne-Cathedral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Wiki-01-Choir-of-Cologne-Cathedral.jpg/440px-Wiki-01-Choir-of-Cologne-Cathedral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1333" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki-01-Choir-of-Cologne-Cathedral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The east end of <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Cathedral" title="Cologne Cathedral">Cologne Cathedral</a> represents the extreme of verticality of Gothic. The choir dates to 13th century and the nave to the 19th century.</div>
</div>
</div>

</div>
</div>
<p>A characteristic of Gothic church architecture is its height, both absolute and in proportion to its width, the verticality suggesting an aspiration to Heaven. A section of the main body of a Gothic church usually shows the <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a> as considerably taller than it is wide. In England the proportion is sometimes greater than 2:1, while the greatest proportional difference achieved is at <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Cathedral" title="Cologne Cathedral">Cologne Cathedral</a> with a ratio of 3.6:1. The highest internal vault is at <a href="/wiki/Beauvais_Cathedral" title="Beauvais Cathedral">Beauvais Cathedral</a> at 48 metres (157&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup> The pointed arch, itself a suggestion of height, is appearance is characteristically further enhanced by both the architectural features and the decoration of the building.<sup id="cite_ref-Swaan_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swaan-55">[49]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salisbury_Cathedral_exterior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Salisbury_Cathedral_exterior.jpg/220px-Salisbury_Cathedral_exterior.jpg" width="220" height="294" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Salisbury_Cathedral_exterior.jpg/330px-Salisbury_Cathedral_exterior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Salisbury_Cathedral_exterior.jpg/440px-Salisbury_Cathedral_exterior.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1712" data-file-height="2288" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salisbury_Cathedral_exterior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a> has the tallest spire in England.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Verticality is emphasised on the exterior in a major way by the <a href="/wiki/Tower" title="Tower">towers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spire" title="Spire">spires</a>, a characteristic of Gothic churches both great and small varying from church to church, and in a lesser way by strongly projecting vertical buttresses, by narrow half-columns called <i>attached shafts</i> which often pass through several storeys of the building, by long narrow windows, vertical mouldings around doors and figurative sculpture which emphasises the vertical and is often attenuated. The roofline, gable ends, buttresses and other parts of the building are often terminated by small pinnacles, <a href="/wiki/Milan_Cathedral" title="Milan Cathedral">Milan Cathedral</a> being an extreme example in the use of this form of decoration. In <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, the tower, if present, is almost always detached from the building, as at <a href="/wiki/Florence_Cathedral" title="Florence Cathedral">Florence Cathedral</a>, and is often from an earlier structure. In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, two towers on the front is the norm. In <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> this is often the arrangement, but an English cathedral may also be surmounted by an enormous tower at the crossing. Smaller churches usually have just one tower, but this may also be the case at larger buildings, such as <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ulm_Minster" title="Ulm Minster">Ulm Minster</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ulm" title="Ulm">Ulm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, completed in 1890 and possessing the tallest spire in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[53]</a></sup> slightly exceeding that of <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Cathedral" title="Lincoln Cathedral">Lincoln Cathedral</a>, the tallest spire that was actually completed during the medieval period, at 160 metres (520&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the interior of the building attached shafts often sweep unbroken from floor to ceiling and meet the ribs of the vault, like a tall tree spreading into branches. The verticals are generally repeated in the treatment of the windows and wall surfaces. In many Gothic churches, particularly in France, and in the <i>Perpendicular period</i> of <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic architecture</a>, the treatment of vertical elements in gallery and window tracery creates a strongly unifying feature that counteracts the horizontal divisions of the interior structure.<sup id="cite_ref-Swaan_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swaan-55">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plan">Plan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Plan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most large Gothic churches and many smaller parish churches are of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin cross">Latin cross</a> (or "cruciform") plan, with a long nave making the body of the church, a transverse arm called the <a href="/wiki/Transept" title="Transept">transept</a> and, beyond it, an extension which may be called the <a href="/wiki/Choir_(architecture)" title="Choir (architecture)">choir</a>, chancel or presbytery. There are several regional variations on this plan.</p>
<p>The nave is generally flanked on either side by aisles, usually single, but sometimes double. The nave is generally considerably taller than the aisles, having <a href="/wiki/Clerestory" title="Clerestory">clerestory</a> windows which light the central space. Gothic churches of the Germanic tradition, like <a href="/wiki/Stephansdom_(Vienna)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephansdom (Vienna)">St. Stephen of Vienna</a>, often have nave and aisles of similar height and are called <i><a href="/wiki/Hall_church" title="Hall church">Hallenkirche</a></i>. In the South of France there is often a single wide nave and no aisles, as at Sainte-Marie in <a href="/wiki/Saint-Bertrand-de-Comminges" title="Saint-Bertrand-de-Comminges">Saint-Bertrand-de-Comminges</a>.</p>
<p>In some churches with double aisles, like <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame,_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame, Paris">Notre Dame, Paris</a>, the transept does not project beyond the aisles. In English cathedrals transepts tend to project boldly and there may be two of them, as at <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a>, though this is not the case with lesser churches.</p>
<p>The eastern arm shows considerable diversity. In England it is generally long and may have two distinct sections, both choir and presbytery. It is often square ended or has a projecting <i>Lady Chapel</i>, dedicated to the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>. In France the eastern end is often polygonal and surrounded by a walkway called an ambulatory and sometimes a ring of chapels called a "chevet." While German churches are often similar to those of France, in Italy, the eastern projection beyond the transept is usually just a shallow apsidal chapel containing the sanctuary, as at <a href="/wiki/Florence_Cathedral" title="Florence Cathedral">Florence Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NP_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NP-39">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swaan_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swaan-55">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another very characteristic feature of the Gothic style, domestic and ecclesiastical alike, is the division of interior space into individual cells according to the building's ribbing and vaults, regardless of whether or not the structure actually has a vaulted ceiling. This system of cells of varying size and shape juxtaposed in various patterns was again totally unique to antiquity and the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a> and scholars, Frankl included, have emphasised the mathematical and geometric nature of this design. Frankl in particular thought of this layout as "creation by division" rather than the <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a>'s "creation by addition." Others, namely <a href="/wiki/Viollet-le-Duc" class="mw-redirect" title="Viollet-le-Duc">Viollet-le-Duc</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Wilhelm_Pinder&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wilhelm Pinder (page does not exist)">Wilhelm Pinder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/August_Schmarsow" title="August Schmarsow">August Schmarsow</a>, instead proposed the term "articulated architecture."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714-62">[55]</a></sup> The opposite theory as suggested by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Focillon" title="Henri Focillon">Henri Focillon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Bony" title="Jean Bony">Jean Bony</a> is of "spacial unification", or of the creation of an interior that is made for sensory overload via the interaction of many elements and perspectives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714.2C_17_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714.2C_17-63">[56]</a></sup> Interior and exterior partitions, often extensively studied, have been found to at times contain features, such as thoroughfares at window height, that make the illusion of thickness. Additionally, the piers separating the isles eventually stopped being part of the walls but rather independent objects that jut out from the actual aisle wall itself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197717_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197717-64">[57]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
