<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Light_and_windows">Light and windows</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Light and windows">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sainte_chapelle_-_Upper_level.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Sainte_chapelle_-_Upper_level.jpg/250px-Sainte_chapelle_-_Upper_level.jpg" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Sainte_chapelle_-_Upper_level.jpg/375px-Sainte_chapelle_-_Upper_level.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Sainte_chapelle_-_Upper_level.jpg/500px-Sainte_chapelle_-_Upper_level.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1330" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sainte_chapelle_-_Upper_level.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sainte_Chapelle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sainte Chapelle">Sainte Chapelle</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One of the most ubiquitous elements of Gothic architecture is the shrinking of the walls and inserting of large windows. Notables such as Viollet-le-Duc, Focillon, Aubert, and <a href="/wiki/Max_Dvo%C5%99%C3%A1k" title="Max Dvořák">Max Dvořák</a> contended that this is one of the most universal features of the Gothic style. Yet another departure from the <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> style, windows grew in size as the Gothic style evolved, eventually almost eliminating all the wall-space as in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Chapelle" title="Sainte-Chapelle">Sainte-Chapelle</a>, admitting immense amounts of light into the church. This expansive interior light has been a feature of Gothic cathedrals since their inception, and this is because of the function of space in a Gothic cathedral as a function of light that is very widely referred to in contemporary text.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197720-16">[12]</a></sup> The metaphysics of light in the Middle Ages led to clerical belief in its divinity and the importance of its display in holy settings. Much of this belief was based on the writings of <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Dionysius" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudo-Dionysius">Pseudo-Dionysius</a>, a 6th-century mystic whose book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Celestial_Hierarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="The Celestial Hierarchy">The Celestial Hierarchy</a></i>, was popular among monks in France. Pseudo-Dionysius held that all light, even light reflected from metals or streamed through windows, was divine. To promote such faith, the abbot in charge of the Saint-Denis church on the north edge of Paris, the <a href="/wiki/Abbot_Suger" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbot Suger">Abbot Suger</a>, encouraged architects remodeling the building to make the interior as bright as possible.</p>
<p>Ever since the remodeled <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint-Denis" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of Saint-Denis">Basilica of Saint-Denis</a> opened in 1144, Gothic architecture has featured expansive windows, such as at <a href="/wiki/Sainte_Chapelle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sainte Chapelle">Sainte Chapelle</a>, <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York Minster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gloucester_Cathedral" title="Gloucester Cathedral">Gloucester Cathedral</a>. The increase in size between windows of the Romanesque and Gothic periods is related to the use of the ribbed vault, and in particular, the pointed ribbed vault which channeled the weight to a supporting shaft with less outward thrust than a semicircular vault. Walls did not need to be so weighty.<sup id="cite_ref-NP_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NP-39">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swaan_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swaan-55">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>A further development was the flying buttress which arched externally from the springing of the vault across the roof of the aisle to a large buttress pier projecting well beyond the line of the external wall. These piers were often surmounted by a <a href="/wiki/Pinnacle" title="Pinnacle">pinnacle</a> or <a href="/wiki/Statue" title="Statue">statue</a>, further adding to the downward weight, and counteracting the outward thrust of the vault and buttress arch as well as stress from wind loading.</p>
<p>The internal columns of the arcade with their attached shafts, the ribs of the vault and the flying buttresses, with their associated vertical buttresses jutting at right-angles to the building, created a stone skeleton. Between these parts, the walls and the infill of the vaults could be of lighter construction. Between the narrow buttresses, the walls could be opened up into large windows.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Through the Gothic period, thanks to the versatility of the pointed arch, the structure of Gothic windows developed from simple openings to immensely rich and decorative sculptural designs. The windows were very often filled with <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a> which added a dimension of colour to the light within the building, as well as providing a medium for figurative and narrative art.<sup id="cite_ref-Swaan_55-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swaan-55">[49]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:060806-France-Paris-Notre_Dame.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/060806-France-Paris-Notre_Dame.jpg/220px-060806-France-Paris-Notre_Dame.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/060806-France-Paris-Notre_Dame.jpg/330px-060806-France-Paris-Notre_Dame.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/060806-France-Paris-Notre_Dame.jpg/440px-060806-France-Paris-Notre_Dame.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:060806-France-Paris-Notre_Dame.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame de Paris</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Majesty">Majesty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Majesty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The façade of a large church or cathedral, often referred to as the <i>West Front</i>, is generally designed to create a powerful impression on the approaching worshipper, demonstrating both the might of God and the might of the institution that it represents. One of the best known and most typical of such façades is that of Notre Dame de Paris.</p>
<p>Central to the façade is the main portal, often flanked by additional doors. In the arch of the door, the <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a>, is often a significant piece of sculpture, most frequently <i>Christ in Majesty</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Judgment Day</a></i>. If there is a central doorjamb or a <a href="/wiki/Trumeau_(architecture)" title="Trumeau (architecture)">trumeau</a>, then it frequently bears a statue of the <i>Madonna and Child</i>. There may be much other carving, often of figures in niches set into the mouldings around the portals, or in sculptural screens extending across the façade.</p>
<p>Above the main portal there is generally a large window, like that at <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York Minster</a>, or a group of windows such as those at <a href="/wiki/Ripon_Cathedral" title="Ripon Cathedral">Ripon Cathedral</a>. In France there is generally a <a href="/wiki/Rose_window" title="Rose window">rose window</a> like that at <a href="/wiki/Reims_Cathedral" title="Reims Cathedral">Reims Cathedral</a>. Rose windows are also often found in the façades of churches of Spain and Italy, but are rarer elsewhere and are not found on the façades of any English Cathedrals. The gable is usually richly decorated with arcading or sculpture or, in the case of Italy, may be decorated with the rest of the façade, with polychrome marble and mosaic, as at <a href="/wiki/Orvieto_Cathedral" title="Orvieto Cathedral">Orvieto Cathedral</a>.</p>
<p>The West Front of a French cathedral and many English, Spanish and German cathedrals generally have two towers, which, particularly in France, express an enormous diversity of form and decoration.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JH1_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JH1-15">[11]</a></sup> However some German cathedrals have only one tower located in the middle of the façade (such as <a href="/wiki/Freiburg_M%C3%BCnster" class="mw-redirect" title="Freiburg Münster">Freiburg Münster</a>).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RiponCathedralFacade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/RiponCathedralFacade.jpg/220px-RiponCathedralFacade.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/RiponCathedralFacade.jpg/330px-RiponCathedralFacade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/RiponCathedralFacade.jpg/440px-RiponCathedralFacade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RiponCathedralFacade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ripon_Cathedral" title="Ripon Cathedral">Ripon Cathedral</a>'s façade presents a composition of pointed arches without tracery.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_shapes_of_Gothic_arches_and_stylistic_character">Basic shapes of Gothic arches and stylistic character</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Basic shapes of Gothic arches and stylistic character">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The way in which the pointed arch was drafted and utilised developed throughout the Gothic period. There were fairly clear stages of development which did not progress at the same rate or in the same way in every country. Moreover, the names used to define various periods or styles within Gothic architecture differs from country to country. The work of art historians <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hans_R._Hahnloser&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hans R. Hahnloser (page does not exist)">Hans R. Hahnloser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Branner" title="Robert Branner">Robert Branner</a> in studying manuscripts and architectural drawings showed that the use of geometric shapes and proportions in squares, circles, semi-circular shapes, and equilateral triangles, abandoned in the Renaissance, was a constant effort in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714-62">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Transverse arches, perpendicular to the upper level of the walls and hidden under gallery roofing, appeared circa 1100 at <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham Cathedral</a> and at <a href="/w/index.php?title=C%C3%A9risy-la-For%C3%AAt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cérisy-la-Forêt (page does not exist)">Cérisy-la-Forêt</a> and are thought to have been used to facilitate roofing and the construction of wall buttressing, as there was no need to give any further support to already thick Romanesque walls.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197739_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197739-43">[39]</a></sup> Used at the nave of Durham and at <a href="/wiki/Caen" title="Caen">Caen</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Sainte-Trinit%C3%A9,_Caen" title="Abbey of Sainte-Trinité, Caen">Abbey of Saint-Trinité</a>, this practice would also be used by Gothic architects at <a href="/wiki/Saint-Germer-de-Fly_Abbey" title="Saint-Germer-de-Fly Abbey">Saint-Germer-de-Fly Abbey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laon_Cathedral" title="Laon Cathedral">Laon Cathedral</a>. Further application and refinement of this technique since the 11th century made the purpose of the transverse clearer, culminating in the late 12th century as architects used its gallery to buttress the upper echelons of a church.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197739_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197739-43">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lancet_arch">Lancet arch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Lancet arch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The simplest shape is the long opening with a pointed arch known in England as the lancet. Lancet openings are often grouped, usually as a cluster of three or five. Lancet openings may be very narrow and steeply pointed. Lancet arches are typically defined as two-centered arches whose radii are larger than the arch's span.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChing20126_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChing20126-65">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a> is famous for the beauty and simplicity of its Lancet Gothic, known in England as the Early English Style. <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York Minster</a> has a group of <a href="/wiki/Lancet_window" title="Lancet window">lancet windows</a> each fifty feet high and still containing ancient glass. They are known as the Five Sisters. These simple undecorated grouped windows are found at Chartres and <a href="/wiki/Laon_Cathedral" title="Laon Cathedral">Laon Cathedrals</a> and are used extensively in <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture_in_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Gothic architecture in Italy">Italy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CT_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CT-21">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:YorkMinsterChapterHouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/YorkMinsterChapterHouse.jpg/220px-YorkMinsterChapterHouse.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/YorkMinsterChapterHouse.jpg/330px-YorkMinsterChapterHouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/YorkMinsterChapterHouse.jpg/440px-YorkMinsterChapterHouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:YorkMinsterChapterHouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Windows in the Chapter House at <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York Minster</a> show the equilateral arch with typical circular motifs in the tracery.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Equilateral_arch">Equilateral arch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Equilateral arch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Equlateral_arch.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/54/Equlateral_arch.PNG/220px-Equlateral_arch.PNG" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/54/Equlateral_arch.PNG 1.5x" data-file-width="290" data-file-height="203" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Equlateral_arch.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Equilateral Arch</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many Gothic openings are based upon the <a href="/wiki/Equilateral_triangle" title="Equilateral triangle">equilateral</a> form. In other words, when the arch is drafted, the <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> is exactly the width of the opening and the centre of each arch coincides with the point from which the opposite arch springs. This makes the arch higher in relation to its width than a semi-circular arch which is exactly half as high as it is wide.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Equilateral Arch gives a wide opening of satisfying proportion useful for doorways, decorative arcades and large windows.</p>
<p>The structural beauty of the Gothic arch means, however, that no set proportion had to be rigidly maintained. The Equilateral Arch was employed as a useful tool, not as a principle of design. This meant that narrower or wider arches were introduced into a building plan wherever necessity dictated. In the architecture of some Italian cities, notably <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, semi-circular arches are interspersed with pointed ones.</p>
<p>The Equilateral Arch lends itself to filling with tracery of simple equilateral, circular and semi-circular forms. The type of tracery that evolved to fill these spaces is known in England as Geometric Decorated Gothic and can be seen to splendid effect at many English and French Cathedrals, notably Lincoln and Notre Dame in Paris. Windows of complex design and of three or more <i>lights</i> or vertical sections, are often designed by overlapping two or more equilateral arches.<sup id="cite_ref-CT_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CT-21">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flamboyant_arch">Flamboyant arch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Flamboyant arch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Limoges_curvilinear_tracery.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Limoges_curvilinear_tracery.JPG/220px-Limoges_curvilinear_tracery.JPG" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Limoges_curvilinear_tracery.JPG/330px-Limoges_curvilinear_tracery.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Limoges_curvilinear_tracery.JPG/440px-Limoges_curvilinear_tracery.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Limoges_curvilinear_tracery.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Flamboyant" title="Flamboyant">Flamboyant</a> tracery at <a href="/wiki/Cath%C3%A9drale_Saint-%C3%89tienne_de_Limoges" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathédrale Saint-Étienne de Limoges">Limoges Cathedral</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Flamboyant" title="Flamboyant">Flamboyant</a> Arch is one that is drafted from four points, the upper part of each main arc turning upwards into a smaller arc and meeting at a sharp, flame-like point. These arches create a rich and lively effect when used for window tracery and surface decoration. The form is structurally weak and has very rarely been used for large openings except when contained within a larger and more stable arch. It is not employed at all for <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaulting</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the most beautiful and famous traceried windows of Europe employ this type of tracery. It can be seen at <a href="/wiki/St._Stephen%27s_Cathedral,_Vienna" title="St. Stephen's Cathedral, Vienna">St Stephen's</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sainte_Chapelle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sainte Chapelle">Sainte Chapelle</a> in Paris, at the Cathedrals of <a href="/wiki/Cath%C3%A9drale_Saint-%C3%89tienne_de_Limoges" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathédrale Saint-Étienne de Limoges">Limoges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rouen_Cathedral" title="Rouen Cathedral">Rouen</a> in France. In England the most famous examples are the West Window of <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York Minster</a> with its design based on the <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Heart" title="Sacred Heart">Sacred Heart</a>, the extraordinarily rich nine-light East Window at <a href="/wiki/Carlisle_Cathedral" title="Carlisle Cathedral">Carlisle Cathedral</a> and the exquisite East window of <a href="/wiki/Selby_Abbey" title="Selby Abbey">Selby Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CT_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CT-21">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NP_39-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NP-39">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Doorways surmounted by Flamboyant mouldings are very common in both ecclesiastical and domestic architecture in France. They are much rarer in England. A notable example is the doorway to the Chapter Room at <a href="/wiki/Rochester_Cathedral" title="Rochester Cathedral">Rochester Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CT_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CT-21">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The style was much used in England for wall arcading and niches. Prime examples in are in the Lady Chapel at <a href="/wiki/Ely_Cathedral" title="Ely Cathedral">Ely</a>, the Screen at <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Cathedral" title="Lincoln Cathedral">Lincoln</a> and externally on the façade of <a href="/wiki/Exeter_Cathedral" title="Exeter Cathedral">Exeter Cathedral</a>. In German and Spanish Gothic architecture it often appears as openwork screens on the exterior of buildings. The style was used to rich and sometimes extraordinary effect in both these countries, notably on the famous pulpit in <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Vienna Cathedral">Vienna Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JH1_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JH1-15">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:F%C3%A4chergew%C3%B6lbe_KingsCollege.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/F%C3%A4chergew%C3%B6lbe_KingsCollege.jpg/220px-F%C3%A4chergew%C3%B6lbe_KingsCollege.jpg" width="220" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/F%C3%A4chergew%C3%B6lbe_KingsCollege.jpg/330px-F%C3%A4chergew%C3%B6lbe_KingsCollege.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/F%C3%A4chergew%C3%B6lbe_KingsCollege.jpg/440px-F%C3%A4chergew%C3%B6lbe_KingsCollege.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2056" data-file-height="2203" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:F%C3%A4chergew%C3%B6lbe_KingsCollege.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The depressed arch supported by fan <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaulting</a> at <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_Chapel" class="mw-redirect" title="King's College Chapel">King's College Chapel</a>, England.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Depressed_arch">Depressed arch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Depressed arch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The depressed or <a href="/wiki/Four-centred_arch" title="Four-centred arch">four-centred arch</a> is much wider than its height and gives the visual effect of having been flattened under pressure. Its structure is achieved by drafting two arcs which rise steeply from each springing point on a small <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> and then turn into two arches with a wide radius and much lower springing point.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>This type of arch, when employed as a window opening, lends itself to very wide spaces, provided it is adequately supported by many narrow vertical shafts. These are often further braced by horizontal transoms. The overall effect produces a grid-like appearance of regular, delicate, rectangular forms with an emphasis on the perpendicular. It is also employed as a wall decoration in which arcade and window openings form part of the whole decorative surface.</p>
<p>The style, known as <a href="/wiki/Perpendicular_Period" class="mw-redirect" title="Perpendicular Period">Perpendicular</a>, that evolved from this treatment is specific to England, although very similar to contemporary Spanish style in particular, and was employed to great effect through the 15th century and first half of the 16th as Renaissance styles were much slower to arrive in England than in Italy and France.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>It can be seen notably at the East End of <a href="/wiki/Gloucester_Cathedral" title="Gloucester Cathedral">Gloucester Cathedral</a> where the East Window is said to be as large as a tennis court. There are three very famous royal chapels and one chapel-like Abbey which show the style at its most elaborate: <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_Chapel" class="mw-redirect" title="King's College Chapel">King's College Chapel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a>; <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Chapel,_Windsor" class="mw-redirect" title="St George's Chapel, Windsor">St George's Chapel, Windsor</a>; <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII%27s_Chapel" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VII's Chapel">Henry VII's Chapel</a> at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bath_Abbey" title="Bath Abbey">Bath Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CT_21-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CT-21">[17]</a></sup> However very many simpler buildings, especially churches built during the wool boom in <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a>, are fine examples of the style.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolism_and_ornamentation">Symbolism and ornamentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Symbolism and ornamentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_architecture_of_Western_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral architecture of Western Europe">Cathedral architecture of Western Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poor_Man%27s_Bible" title="Poor Man's Bible">Poor Man's Bible</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chartres.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Chartres.jpg/250px-Chartres.jpg" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Chartres.jpg/375px-Chartres.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Chartres.jpg/500px-Chartres.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1063" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chartres.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Royal Portal</i> of <a href="/wiki/Chartres_Cathedral" title="Chartres Cathedral">Chartres Cathedral</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Gothic cathedral represented the universe in microcosm and each architectural concept, including the loftiness and huge dimensions of the structure, were intended to convey a theological message: the great glory of God. The building becomes a microcosm in two ways. Firstly, the mathematical and geometrical nature of the construction is an image of the orderly universe, in which an underlying rationality and logic can be perceived.</p>
<p>Secondly, the <a href="/wiki/Statue" title="Statue">statues</a>, sculptural decoration, <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mural" title="Mural">murals</a> incorporate the essence of creation in depictions of the <a href="/wiki/Labours_of_the_Months" title="Labours of the Months">Labours of the Months</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">Zodiac</a><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[h]</a></sup> and sacred history from the Old and New Testaments and Lives of the Saints, as well as reference to the eternal in the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Last Judgment</a> and <i>Coronation of the Virgin</i>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cathedrale-de-Strasbourg-IMG_1199.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Cathedrale-de-Strasbourg-IMG_1199.jpg/250px-Cathedrale-de-Strasbourg-IMG_1199.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Cathedrale-de-Strasbourg-IMG_1199.jpg/375px-Cathedrale-de-Strasbourg-IMG_1199.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Cathedrale-de-Strasbourg-IMG_1199.jpg/500px-Cathedrale-de-Strasbourg-IMG_1199.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cathedrale-de-Strasbourg-IMG_1199.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Devil tempting the <i>Foolish Virgins</i> at <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg_Cathedral" title="Strasbourg Cathedral">Strasbourg</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The decorative schemes usually incorporated <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Biblical</a> stories, emphasizing visual <a href="/wiki/Medieval_allegory" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval allegory">typological allegories</a> between <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> prophecy and the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JH1_15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JH1-15">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many churches were very richly decorated, both inside and out. Sculpture and architectural details were often bright with coloured paint of which traces remain at the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Chartres" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of Chartres">Cathedral of Chartres</a>. Wooden ceilings and panelling were usually brightly coloured. Sometimes the stone columns of the nave were painted, and the panels in decorative wall arcading contained narratives or figures of saints. These have rarely remained intact, but may be seen at the Chapterhouse of <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CT_21-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CT-21">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some important Gothic churches could be severely simple such as the <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">Basilica</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mary_Magdalene" title="Mary Magdalene">Mary Magdalene</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saint-Maximin-la-Sainte-Baume" title="Saint-Maximin-la-Sainte-Baume">Saint-Maximin</a>, Provence where the local traditions of the sober, massive, Romanesque architecture were still strong.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_differences">Regional differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Regional differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_cathedrals_and_great_churches" title="Architecture of cathedrals and great churches">Architecture of cathedrals and great churches</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Picardie_Amiens2_tango7174.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Picardie_Amiens2_tango7174.jpg/220px-Picardie_Amiens2_tango7174.jpg" width="220" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Picardie_Amiens2_tango7174.jpg/330px-Picardie_Amiens2_tango7174.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Picardie_Amiens2_tango7174.jpg/440px-Picardie_Amiens2_tango7174.jpg 2x" data-file-width="727" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Picardie_Amiens2_tango7174.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Interior of <a href="/wiki/Amiens_Cathedral" title="Amiens Cathedral">Amiens Cathedral</a>, France.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wherever Gothic architecture is found, it is subject to local influences, and frequently the influence of itinerant stonemasons and artisans, carrying ideas between cities and sometimes between countries. Certain characteristics are typical of particular regions and often override the style itself, appearing in buildings hundreds of years apart.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/French_Gothic_architecture" title="French Gothic architecture">French Gothic architecture</a></div>
<p>The distinctive characteristic of French,cathedrals, and those in Germany and Belgium that were strongly influenced by them, is their height and their impression of verticality. Each French cathedral tends to be stylistically unified in appearance when compared with an English cathedral where there is great diversity in almost every building. They are compact, with slight or no projection of the transepts and subsidiary chapels. The west fronts are highly consistent, having three portals surmounted by a rose window, and two large towers. Sometimes there are additional towers on the transept ends. The east end is polygonal with ambulatory and sometimes a chevette of radiating chapels. In the south of France, many of the major churches are without transepts and some are without aisles.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="England">England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Interieur_cathedrale_de_wells.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Interieur_cathedrale_de_wells.JPG/250px-Interieur_cathedrale_de_wells.JPG" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Interieur_cathedrale_de_wells.JPG/375px-Interieur_cathedrale_de_wells.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Interieur_cathedrale_de_wells.JPG/500px-Interieur_cathedrale_de_wells.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Interieur_cathedrale_de_wells.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The longitudinal emphasis in the nave of <a href="/wiki/Wells_Cathedral" title="Wells Cathedral">Wells</a> is typically English.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The distinctive characteristic of English cathedrals is their extreme length, and their internal emphasis upon the horizontal, which may be emphasised visually as much or more than the vertical lines. Each English cathedral (with the exception of Salisbury) has an extraordinary degree of stylistic diversity, when compared with most French, German and Italian cathedrals. It is not unusual for every part of the building to have been built in a different century and in a different style, with no attempt at creating a stylistic unity. Unlike French cathedrals, English cathedrals sprawl across their sites, with double transepts projecting strongly and <i>Lady Chapels</i> tacked on at a later date, such as at Westminster Abbey. In the west front, the doors are not as significant as in France, the usual congregational entrance being through a side porch. The West window is very large and never a rose, which are reserved for the transept gables. The west front may have two towers like a French Cathedral, or none. There is nearly always a tower at the crossing and it may be very large and surmounted by a spire. The distinctive English east end is square, but it may take a completely different form. Both internally and externally, the stonework is often richly decorated with carvings, particularly the capitals.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CT_21-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CT-21">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Germany,_Poland_and_the_Czech_lands"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany.2C_Poland_and_the_Czech_lands">Germany, Poland and the Czech lands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Germany, Poland and the Czech lands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Polish_Gothic_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish Gothic architecture">Polish Gothic architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Czech_Gothic_architecture" title="Czech Gothic architecture">Czech Gothic architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Prag_Vladislav-Saal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Prag_Vladislav-Saal.jpg/220px-Prag_Vladislav-Saal.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Prag_Vladislav-Saal.jpg/330px-Prag_Vladislav-Saal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Prag_Vladislav-Saal.jpg/440px-Prag_Vladislav-Saal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1932" data-file-height="2576" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Prag_Vladislav-Saal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Interior of the <a href="/wiki/Vladislav_Hall" title="Vladislav Hall">Vladislav Hall</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Prague_Castle" title="Prague Castle">Prague Castle</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Romanesque architecture in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> (earlier called <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>) is characterised by its massive and modular nature. This characteristic is also expressed in the Gothic architecture of <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a> in the huge size of the towers and spires, often projected, but not always completed.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[i]</a></sup> Gothic design in Germany and <a href="/wiki/Czech_lands" title="Czech lands">Czech lands</a>, generally follows the French formula, but the towers are much taller and, if complete, are surmounted by enormous openwork spires that are a regional feature. Because of the size of the towers, the section of the façade between them may appear narrow and compressed. The distinctive character of the interior of German Gothic cathedrals is their breadth and openness. This is the case even when, as at Cologne, they have been modelled upon a French cathedral. German and Czech cathedrals, like the French, tend not to have strongly projecting <a href="/wiki/Transept" title="Transept">transepts</a>. There are also many <a href="/wiki/Hall_church" title="Hall church">hall churches</a> (<i>Hallenkirchen</i>) without clerestory windows.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swaan_55-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swaan-55">[49]</a></sup> In contrast to the Gothic designs found in western German and Czech areas, which followed the French patterns, <a href="/wiki/Brick_Gothic" title="Brick Gothic">Brick Gothic</a> was particularly prevalent in Poland and northern Germany. The Polish gothic architecture is characterised by its utilitarian nature, with very limited use of sculpture and heavy exterior design.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain_and_Portugal">Spain and Portugal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Spain and Portugal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burgos_Catedral_Triforio.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Burgos_Catedral_Triforio.JPG/250px-Burgos_Catedral_Triforio.JPG" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Burgos_Catedral_Triforio.JPG/375px-Burgos_Catedral_Triforio.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Burgos_Catedral_Triforio.JPG/500px-Burgos_Catedral_Triforio.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burgos_Catedral_Triforio.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Burgos_Cathedral" title="Burgos Cathedral">Burgos Cathedral</a> in Burgos, Spain.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Gothic_architecture" title="Spanish Gothic architecture">Spanish Gothic architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Gothic_architecture" title="Portuguese Gothic architecture">Portuguese Gothic architecture</a></div>
<p>The distinctive characteristic of Gothic cathedrals of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> is their spatial complexity, with many areas of different shapes leading from each other. They are comparatively wide, and often have very tall arcades surmounted by low clerestories, giving a similar spacious appearance to the <i>Hallenkirche</i> of Germany, as at the Church of the <a href="/wiki/Batalha_Monastery" title="Batalha Monastery">Batalha Monastery</a> in Portugal. Many of the cathedrals are completely surrounded by chapels. Like English cathedrals, each is often stylistically diverse. This expresses itself both in the addition of chapels and in the application of decorative details drawn from different sources. Among the influences on both decoration and form are <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a> and, towards the end of the period, Renaissance details combined with the Gothic in a distinctive manner. The West front, as at <a href="/wiki/Leon_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Leon Cathedral">Leon Cathedral</a>, typically resembles a French west front, but wider in proportion to height and often with greater diversity of detail and a combination of intricate ornament with broad plain surfaces. At <a href="/wiki/Burgos_Cathedral" title="Burgos Cathedral">Burgos Cathedral</a> there are spires of German style. The roofline often has pierced parapets with comparatively few pinnacles. There are often towers and domes of a great variety of shapes and structural invention rising above the roof.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crown_of_Aragon">Crown of Aragon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Crown of Aragon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Catalan_Gothic" title="Catalan Gothic">Catalan Gothic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barcelona_catedrale_vista_interno_a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Barcelona_catedrale_vista_interno_a.jpg/250px-Barcelona_catedrale_vista_interno_a.jpg" width="250" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Barcelona_catedrale_vista_interno_a.jpg/375px-Barcelona_catedrale_vista_interno_a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Barcelona_catedrale_vista_interno_a.jpg/500px-Barcelona_catedrale_vista_interno_a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1562" data-file-height="1152" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barcelona_catedrale_vista_interno_a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Barcelona_Cathedral" title="Barcelona Cathedral">Barcelona Cathedral</a> has a wide nave with the clerestory windows nestled under the vault.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">Crown of Aragon</a> and the territories under its influence (Aragon, Catalonia, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Catalonia" title="Northern Catalonia">Northern Catalonia</a> in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Valencian_Country" class="mw-redirect" title="Valencian Country">Valencian Country</a>, among others in the <a href="/wiki/Italia" class="mw-redirect" title="Italia">Italian</a> islands), the Gothic style suppressed the <a href="/wiki/Transept" title="Transept">transept</a> and made the <a href="/wiki/Aisle" title="Aisle">aisle</a> almost as high as the main nave, allowing it to create very wide spaces, with few ornaments; it is called <i>Catalan Gothic style</i> (different than the Kingdom of Castile or French style).</p>
<p>The most important samples of Catalan Gothic style are the cathedrals of <a href="/wiki/Girona" title="Girona">Girona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perpignan" title="Perpignan">Perpignan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palma,_Majorca" title="Palma, Majorca">Palma</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Mallorca" class="mw-redirect" title="Mallorca">Mallorca</a>), the basilica of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_del_Mar,_Barcelona" title="Santa Maria del Mar, Barcelona">Santa Maria del Mar</a> (in Barcelona), the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_del_Pi,_Barcelona" title="Santa Maria del Pi, Barcelona">Basílica del Pi</a> (in Barcelona), and the church of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria,_Manresa" title="Santa Maria, Manresa">Santa Maria de l'Alba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manresa" title="Manresa">Manresa</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Italian_Gothic_architecture" title="Italian Gothic architecture">Italian Gothic architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:StMariadelFiore-inner_a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/StMariadelFiore-inner_a.jpg/250px-StMariadelFiore-inner_a.jpg" width="250" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/StMariadelFiore-inner_a.jpg/375px-StMariadelFiore-inner_a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/StMariadelFiore-inner_a.jpg/500px-StMariadelFiore-inner_a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="820" data-file-height="510" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:StMariadelFiore-inner_a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The clear proportions of <a href="/wiki/Florence_Cathedral" title="Florence Cathedral">Florence Cathedral</a> are defined by dark stone against the colour-washed plastered brick.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The distinctive characteristic of Italian Gothic is the use of polychrome decoration, both externally as marble veneer on the brick façade and also internally where the arches are often made of alternating black and white segments, and where the columns may be painted red, the walls decorated with frescoes and the apse with mosaic. The plan is usually regular and symmetrical, Italian cathedrals have few and widely spaced columns. The proportions are generally mathematically equilibrated, based on the square and the concept of "<i>armonìa</i>," and except in Venice where they loved flamboyant arches, the arches are almost always equilateral. Colours and moldings define the architectural units rather than blending them. Italian cathedral façades are often polychrome and may include mosaics in the lunettes over the doors. The façades have projecting open porches and occular or wheel windows rather than roses, and do not usually have a tower. The crossing is usually surmounted by a dome. There is often a free-standing tower and baptistry. The eastern end usually has an apse of comparatively low projection. The windows are not as large as in northern Europe and, although stained glass windows are often found, the favourite narrative medium for the interior is the <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_14-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-14">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Gothic_buildings">Other Gothic buildings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Other Gothic buildings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gothic_secular_and_domestic_architecture" title="Gothic secular and domestic architecture">Gothic secular and domestic architecture</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">Castle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:D%27s_palace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/D%27s_palace.jpg/250px-D%27s_palace.jpg" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/D%27s_palace.jpg/375px-D%27s_palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/D%27s_palace.jpg/500px-D%27s_palace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:D%27s_palace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Façade of <a href="/wiki/Doge%27s_Palace" title="Doge's Palace">Doge's Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Synagogues were commonly built in the Gothic style in Europe during the Medieval period. A surviving example is the <a href="/wiki/Old_New_Synagogue" title="Old New Synagogue">Old New Synagogue</a> in Prague built in the 13th century.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Palais_des_Papes" title="Palais des Papes">Palais des Papes</a> in Avignon is the best complete large royal palace, alongside the <a href="/wiki/Palacio_Real_de_Olite" class="mw-redirect" title="Palacio Real de Olite">Royal palace of Olite</a>, built during the 13th and 14th centuries for the kings of <a href="/wiki/Navarre" title="Navarre">Navarre</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Malbork_Castle" title="Malbork Castle">Malbork Castle</a> built for the master of the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic order">Teutonic order</a> is an example of <a href="/wiki/Brick_Gothic" title="Brick Gothic">Brick Gothic</a> architecture. Partial survivals of former royal residences include the <a href="/wiki/Doge%27s_Palace" title="Doge's Palace">Doge's Palace</a> of <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Palau_de_la_Generalitat_de_Catalunya" title="Palau de la Generalitat de Catalunya">Palau de la Generalitat</a> in <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>, built in the 15th century for the kings of <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">Aragon</a>, or the famous <a href="/wiki/Conciergerie" title="Conciergerie">Conciergerie</a>, former palace of the kings of France, in Paris.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Palau_de_la_Generalitat_(Barcelona)_-_13.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Palau_de_la_Generalitat_%28Barcelona%29_-_13.jpg/250px-Palau_de_la_Generalitat_%28Barcelona%29_-_13.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Palau_de_la_Generalitat_%28Barcelona%29_-_13.jpg/375px-Palau_de_la_Generalitat_%28Barcelona%29_-_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Palau_de_la_Generalitat_%28Barcelona%29_-_13.jpg/500px-Palau_de_la_Generalitat_%28Barcelona%29_-_13.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="534" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Palau_de_la_Generalitat_(Barcelona)_-_13.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gallery of <a href="/wiki/Palau_de_la_Generalitat" class="mw-redirect" title="Palau de la Generalitat">Palau de la Generalitat</a> in <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Secular Gothic architecture can also be found in a number of public buildings such as <a href="/wiki/Town_halls" class="mw-redirect" title="Town halls">town halls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Universities" class="mw-redirect" title="Universities">universities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Markets" class="mw-redirect" title="Markets">markets</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hospitals" class="mw-redirect" title="Hospitals">hospitals</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocław">Wrocław</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stralsund" title="Stralsund">Stralsund</a> town halls are remarkable examples of northern Brick Gothic built in the late 14th centuries. The <a href="/wiki/Belfry_of_Bruges" title="Belfry of Bruges">Belfry of Bruges</a> or <a href="/wiki/Brussels_Town_Hall" title="Brussels Town Hall">Brussels Town Hall</a>, built during the 15th century, are associated to the increasing wealth and power of the bourgeoisie in the late Middle Ages; by the 15th century, the traders of the trade cities of <a href="/wiki/Burgundy" title="Burgundy">Burgundy</a> had acquired such wealth and influence that they could afford to express their power by funding lavishly decorated buildings of vast proportions. This kind of expressions of secular and economic power are also found in other late mediaeval commercial cities, including the <a href="/wiki/Llotja_de_la_Seda" title="Llotja de la Seda">Llotja de la Seda</a> of <a href="/wiki/Valencia" title="Valencia">Valencia</a>, Spain, a purpose built silk exchange dating from the 15th century, in the partial remains of <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster#Westminster_Hall" title="Palace of Westminster">Westminster Hall</a> in the Houses of Parliament in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Pubblico" title="Palazzo Pubblico">Palazzo Pubblico</a> in <a href="/wiki/Siena" title="Siena">Siena</a>, Italy, a 13th-century town hall built to host the offices of the then prosperous republic of <a href="/wiki/Siena" title="Siena">Siena</a>. Other Italian cities such as <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> (<a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Vecchio" title="Palazzo Vecchio">Palazzo Vecchio</a>), <a href="/wiki/Mantua" title="Mantua">Mantua</a> or <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> also host remarkable examples of secular public architecture.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Collegium_Maius_07.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Collegium_Maius_07.JPG/250px-Collegium_Maius_07.JPG" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Collegium_Maius_07.JPG/375px-Collegium_Maius_07.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Collegium_Maius_07.JPG/500px-Collegium_Maius_07.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Collegium_Maius_07.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Courtyard of <a href="/wiki/Collegium_Maius" title="Collegium Maius">Collegium Maius</a> in <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the late Middle Ages university towns had grown in wealth and importance as well, and this was reflected in the buildings of some of Europe's ancient universities. Particularly remarkable examples still standing nowadays include the <a href="/wiki/Collegio_di_Spagna" title="Collegio di Spagna">Collegio di Spagna</a> in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a>, built during the 14th and 15th centuries; the <a href="/wiki/Karolinum" title="Karolinum">Collegium Carolinum</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Charles_University_in_Prague" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles University in Prague">University of Prague</a> in Bohemia; the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Salamanca" title="University of Salamanca">Escuelas mayores</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Salamanca" title="University of Salamanca">University of Salamanca</a> in Spain; the chapel of <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College,_Cambridge" title="King's College, Cambridge">King's College, Cambridge</a>; or the <a href="/wiki/Collegium_Maius" title="Collegium Maius">Collegium Maius</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">Jagiellonian University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>, Poland.</p>
<p>In addition to monumental secular architecture, examples of the Gothic style in private buildings can be seen in surviving medieval portions of cities across Europe, above all the distinctive <a href="/wiki/Venetian_Gothic_architecture" title="Venetian Gothic architecture">Venetian Gothic</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Ca%27_d%27Oro" title="Ca' d'Oro">Ca' d'Oro</a>. The house of the wealthy early 15th century merchant <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Coeur" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacques Coeur">Jacques Coeur</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bourges" title="Bourges">Bourges</a>, is the classic Gothic bourgeois mansion, full of the asymmetry and complicated detail beloved of the Gothic Revival.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other cities with a concentration of secular Gothic include <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siena" title="Siena">Siena</a>. Most surviving small secular buildings are relatively plain and straightforward; most windows are flat-topped with <a href="/wiki/Mullion" title="Mullion">mullions</a>, with pointed arches and vaulted ceilings often only found at a few focal points. The country-houses of the nobility were slow to abandon the appearance of being a castle, even in parts of Europe, like England, where defence had ceased to be a real concern. The living and working parts of many monastic buildings survive, for example at <a href="/wiki/Mont_Saint-Michel" title="Mont Saint-Michel">Mont Saint-Michel</a>.</p>
<p>Exceptional works of Gothic architecture can also be found on the islands of <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, in the walled cities of <a href="/wiki/Nicosia" title="Nicosia">Nicosia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Famagusta" title="Famagusta">Famagusta</a>. Also, the roofs of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Town_Hall_(Prague)" title="Old Town Hall (Prague)">Old Town Hall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> and <a href="/wiki/Znojmo_Town_Hall_Tower" title="Znojmo Town Hall Tower">Znojmo Town Hall Tower</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> are an excellent example of late Gothic craftsmanship.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gothic_survival_and_revival">Gothic survival and revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Gothic survival and revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Gothic Revival architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/220px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/330px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Westminster_abbey_west.jpg/440px-Westminster_abbey_west.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster_abbey_west.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Western façade of <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, London, completed in 1745</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1663 at the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>'s residence, <a href="/wiki/Lambeth_Palace" title="Lambeth Palace">Lambeth Palace</a>, a Gothic <a href="/wiki/Hammerbeam_roof" title="Hammerbeam roof">hammerbeam roof</a> was built to replace that destroyed when the building was sacked during the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>. Also in the late 17th century, some discrete Gothic details appeared on new construction at <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a>, notably on <a href="/wiki/Tom_Tower" title="Tom Tower">Tom Tower</a> at <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church,_Oxford" title="Christ Church, Oxford">Christ Church, Oxford</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">Christopher Wren</a>. It is not easy to decide whether these instances were <i>Gothic survival</i> or early appearances of <i>Gothic revival</i>.</p>
<p>Ireland was a focus for Gothic architecture in the 17th and 18th centuries. <a href="/wiki/St_Columb%27s_Cathedral" title="St Columb's Cathedral">Derry Cathedral</a> (completed 1633), <a href="/wiki/St_John_the_Baptist_Cathedral,_Sligo" title="St John the Baptist Cathedral, Sligo">Sligo Cathedral</a> (c. 1730), and <a href="/wiki/Down_Cathedral" title="Down Cathedral">Down Cathedral</a> (1790-1818) are notable examples. The term "Planter's Gothic" has been applied to the most typical of these.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>In England in the mid-18th century, the Gothic style was more widely revived, first as a decorative, whimsical alternative to <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a> that is still conventionally termed 'Gothick', of which <a href="/wiki/Horace_Walpole" title="Horace Walpole">Horace Walpole</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Twickenham" title="Twickenham">Twickenham</a> <a href="/wiki/Villa" title="Villa">villa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strawberry_Hill_House" title="Strawberry Hill House">Strawberry Hill</a>, is the familiar example.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gothic_Revival">Gothic Revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Gothic Revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Big_Ben_1_2012-07-03.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Big_Ben_1_2012-07-03.jpg/220px-Big_Ben_1_2012-07-03.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Big_Ben_1_2012-07-03.jpg/330px-Big_Ben_1_2012-07-03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Big_Ben_1_2012-07-03.jpg/440px-Big_Ben_1_2012-07-03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="4608" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Big_Ben_1_2012-07-03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Big_Ben" title="Big Ben">Elizabeth Tower (Big Ben)</a> (completed in 1859) and the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Houses of Parliament</a> in London</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The middle of the 19th century was a period marked by the restoration, and in some cases modification, of ancient monuments and the construction of Neo-Gothic edifices such as the nave of <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Cathedral" title="Cologne Cathedral">Cologne Cathedral</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Clotilde,_Paris" title="Sainte-Clotilde, Paris">Sainte-Clotilde</a> of <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> as speculation of medieval architecture turned to technical consideration. London’s Palace of Westminster, St. Pancras railway station, New York’s Trinity Church and St. Patrick’s Cathedral are also famous examples of Gothic Revival buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>While some credit for this new ideation can reasonably be assigned to German and English writers, namely <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Vetter" title="Johannes Vetter">Johannes Vetter</a>, Franz Mertens, and Robert Willis respectively, this emerging style's champion was <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a>, whose lead was taken by archaeologists, historians, and architects like <a href="/wiki/Jules_Quicherat" title="Jules Quicherat">Jules Quicherat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Choisy" title="Auguste Choisy">Auguste Choisy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Aubert" title="Marcel Aubert">Marcel Aubert</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197711_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197711-8">[6]</a></sup> In the last years of the 19th century, a trend among study in art history emerged in Germany that a building, as defined by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Focillon" title="Henri Focillon">Henri Focillon</a> was an interpretation of space.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197713_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197713-54">[48]</a></sup> When applied to Gothic cathedrals, historians and architects used to the dimensions of 17th and 18th <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical</a> structures, were astounded by the height and extreme length of the cathedrals compared to its proportionally modest width. <a href="/wiki/Goethe" class="mw-redirect" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>, in the preceding century, was mesmerised by the space within a Gothic church and succeeding historians like <a href="/wiki/Dehio" class="mw-disambig" title="Dehio">Dehio</a><sup class="plainlinks noprint Inline-Template" style="vertical-align:text-top;white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://dispenser.homenet.org/~dispenser/cgi-bin/dab_solver.py?page=Gothic_architecture&amp;editintro=Template:Disambiguation_needed/editintro&amp;client=Template:Dn"><span title="Link needs disambiguation. (August 2017)">disambiguation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, Walter Ueberwasser, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Frankl" title="Paul Frankl">Paul Frankl</a>, and Maria Velte sought to rediscover the methodology used in their construction by making measurements and drawings of the buildings, and reading and making conjectures from documents and treaties pertaining to their construction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrodecki197714-62">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In England, partly in response to a philosophy propounded by the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Movement" title="Oxford Movement">Oxford Movement</a> and others associated with the emerging revival of 'high church' or <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Catholic">Anglo-Catholic</a> ideas during the second quarter of the 19th century, neo-Gothic began to become promoted by influential establishment figures as the preferred style for ecclesiastical, civic and institutional architecture. The appeal of this <a href="/wiki/Gothic_revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Gothic revival">Gothic revival</a> (which after 1837, in Britain, is sometimes termed <a href="/wiki/Victorian_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="Victorian Gothic">Victorian Gothic</a>), gradually widened to encompass "low church" as well as "high church" clients. This period of more universal appeal, spanning 1855–1885, is known in Britain as <a href="/wiki/High_Victorian_Gothic" title="High Victorian Gothic">High Victorian Gothic</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Houses of Parliament</a> in London by Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Barry" title="Charles Barry">Charles Barry</a> with interiors by a major exponent of the early Gothic Revival, <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Welby_Pugin" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustus Welby Pugin">Augustus Welby Pugin</a>, is an example of the Gothic revival style from its earlier period in the second quarter of the 19th century. Examples from the <i>High Victorian Gothic</i> period include <a href="/wiki/George_Gilbert_Scott" title="George Gilbert Scott">George Gilbert Scott</a>'s design for the <a href="/wiki/Albert_Memorial" title="Albert Memorial">Albert Memorial</a> in London, and <a href="/wiki/William_Butterfield" title="William Butterfield">William Butterfield</a>'s chapel at <a href="/wiki/Keble_College,_Oxford" title="Keble College, Oxford">Keble College, Oxford</a>. From the second half of the 19th century onwards it became more common in Britain for neo-Gothic to be used in the design of non-ecclesiastical and non-governmental buildings types. Gothic details even began to appear in working-class housing schemes subsidised by philanthropy, though given the expense, less frequently than in the design of upper and middle-class housing.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gassonsummer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gassonsummer.jpg/220px-Gassonsummer.jpg" width="220" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gassonsummer.jpg/330px-Gassonsummer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gassonsummer.jpg/440px-Gassonsummer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="614" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gassonsummer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gasson_Hall" title="Gasson Hall">Gasson Hall</a> on the campus of <a href="/wiki/Boston_College" title="Boston College">Boston College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chestnut_Hill,_Massachusetts" title="Chestnut Hill, Massachusetts">Chestnut Hill</a>, Massachusetts.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In France, simultaneously, the towering figure of the Gothic Revival was <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a>, who outdid historical Gothic constructions to create a Gothic as it ought to have been, notably at the fortified city of <a href="/wiki/Carcassonne" title="Carcassonne">Carcassonne</a> in the south of France and in some richly fortified keeps for industrial magnates. Viollet-le-Duc compiled and coordinated an <i>Encyclopédie médiévale</i> that was a rich repertory his contemporaries mined for architectural details. He effected vigorous restoration of crumbling detail of French cathedrals, including the Abbey of Saint-Denis and famously at Notre Dame de Paris, where many of whose most "Gothic" gargoyles are Viollet-le-Duc's. He taught a generation of reform-Gothic designers and showed how to apply Gothic style to modern structural materials, especially <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AlexandriaCapella.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/AlexandriaCapella.jpg/250px-AlexandriaCapella.jpg" width="250" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/AlexandriaCapella.jpg/375px-AlexandriaCapella.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/AlexandriaCapella.jpg/500px-AlexandriaCapella.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1558" data-file-height="1164" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AlexandriaCapella.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
St. Alexander Nevsky <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Chapel_(Peterhof)" title="Gothic Chapel (Peterhof)">Gothic Chapel (Peterhof)</a>, completed in 1834</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Germany, the great cathedral of <a href="/wiki/Cologne_cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Cologne cathedral">Cologne</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ulm_Minster" title="Ulm Minster">Ulm Minster</a>, left unfinished for 600 years, were brought to completion, while in Italy, <a href="/wiki/Florence_Cathedral" title="Florence Cathedral">Florence Cathedral</a> finally received its polychrome Gothic façade. New churches in the Gothic style were created all over the world, including Mexico, <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_La_Plata" title="Cathedral of La Plata">Argentina</a>, Japan, Thailand, India, Australia, New Zealand, Hawaii and South Africa.</p>
<p>As in Europe, the United States, Canada, Australia and New Zealand utilised Neo-Gothic for the building of universities, a fine example being the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sydney" title="University of Sydney">University of Sydney</a> by <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Blacket" title="Edmund Blacket">Edmund Blacket</a>. In Canada, the Canadian <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Hill" title="Parliament Hill">Parliament Buildings</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a> designed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Fuller_(architect)" title="Thomas Fuller (architect)">Thomas Fuller</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chilion_Jones" title="Chilion Jones">Chilion Jones</a> with its huge centrally placed tower is influenced by Flemish Gothic buildings.</p>
<p>Although falling out of favour for domestic and civic use, Gothic for churches and universities continued into the 20th century with buildings such as <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_Cathedral" title="Liverpool Cathedral">Liverpool Cathedral</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Saint_John_the_Divine" title="Cathedral of Saint John the Divine">Cathedral of Saint John the Divine</a>, New York and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_Cathedral" title="São Paulo Cathedral">São Paulo Cathedral</a>, Brazil. The Gothic style was also applied to iron-framed city skyscrapers such as <a href="/wiki/Cass_Gilbert" title="Cass Gilbert">Cass Gilbert</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Woolworth_Building" title="Woolworth Building">Woolworth Building</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Hood" title="Raymond Hood">Raymond Hood</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Tribune_Tower" title="Tribune Tower">Tribune Tower</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Post-Modernism" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-Modernism">Post-Modernism</a> in the late 20th and early 21st centuries has seen some revival of Gothic forms in individual buildings, such as the <a href="/wiki/Gare_do_Oriente" title="Gare do Oriente">Gare do Oriente</a> in Lisbon, Portugal and a finishing of the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Our_Lady_of_Guadalupe" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of Our Lady of Guadalupe">Cathedral of Our Lady of Guadalupe</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
